      <p><b>Yemen</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/É/: 'e' in 'dress'">É</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/Én/: 'on' in 'button'">Én</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Yemen.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/En-us-Yemen.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Yemen.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ù±ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙ</span>â, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-Yaman</i>), officially the <b>Republic of Yemen</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ù±ÙÙØ¬ÙÙÙÙÙÙØ±ÙÙÙÙØ©Ù Ù±ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙØ©Ù</span>â, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-JumhÅ«rÄ«yah al-YamanÄ«yah</i>, literally "Yemeni Republic"), is a country at the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>. It is the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> in the peninsula, occupying 527,970 square kilometres (203,850 square miles). The coastline stretches for about 2,000 kilometres (1,200 miles).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Yemen is characterized as a <a href="/wiki/Failed_state" title="Failed state">failed state</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> with high necessity of transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Yemen's constitutionally stated capital is the city of <a href="/wiki/Sanaa" title="Sanaa">Sanaa</a>, but the city has been under <a href="/wiki/Houthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Houthis">Houthi</a> <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Civil_War_(2015%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni Civil War (2015âpresent)">rebel control</a> since February 2015 as well as <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a>, which is also controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Transitional_Council" title="Southern Transitional Council">Southern Transitional Council</a> since 2018. Its executive administration resides in <a href="/wiki/Riyadh" title="Riyadh">Riyadh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. </p>
      <p>In ancient times, Yemen was the home of the <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaeans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Burrowes2010_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrowes2010-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Simpson2002_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson2002-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitchen2003_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchen2003-14">[14]</a></sup> a trading state that included parts of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>. In 275 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, later the <a href="/wiki/Yemenite_Jews" title="Yemenite Jews">Jewish</a>-influenced <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> arrived in the fourth century. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> spread quickly in the seventh century and Yemenite troops were crucial in the early Islamic conquests.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The administration of Yemen has long been notoriously difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasties</a> emerged in the 9th to 16th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_dynasty" title="Rasulid dynasty">Rasulid dynasty</a> being the most successful. The country was divided between the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> empires in the 1800s. The <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaydi</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutawakkilite_Kingdom_of_Yemen" title="Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen">Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen</a> was established after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mutawakkilite_Kingdom_of_Yemen" title="Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen">Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen</a> before the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a> in 1962. <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a> remained a <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorate</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Aden_Protectorate" title="Aden Protectorate">Aden Protectorate</a> until 1967 when it became an independent state and later, a <a href="/wiki/Marxist-Leninist_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist-Leninist state">Marxist-Leninist state</a>. The two Yemeni states <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_unification" title="Yemeni unification">united</a> to form the modern Republic of Yemen (<i>al-JumhÅ«rÄ«yah al-YamanÄ«yah</i>) in 1990. President <a href="/wiki/Ali_Abdullah_Saleh" title="Ali Abdullah Saleh">Ali Abdullah Saleh</a> was the first president of the new republic until his resignation in 2012 in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2011, Yemen has been in a state of <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Crisis_(2011%E2%80%93present)" title="Yemeni Crisis (2011âpresent)">political crisis</a> starting with <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Revolution" title="Yemeni Revolution">street protests</a> against poverty, unemployment, corruption, and president Saleh's plan to amend <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Yemen" title="Constitution of Yemen">Yemen's constitution</a> and eliminate the presidential term limit, in effect making him <a href="/wiki/President_for_life" title="President for life">president for life</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gelvin_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gelvin-20">[20]</a></sup> President Saleh stepped down and the powers of the presidency were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Abdrabbuh_Mansur_Hadi" title="Abdrabbuh Mansur Hadi">Abdrabbuh Mansur Hadi</a>. Since then, the country has been in a <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Civil_War_(2015%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni Civil War (2015âpresent)">civil war</a> (alongside the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi Arabian-led military intervention</a> aimed at restoring Hadi's government) with several proto-state entities claiming to govern Yemen: the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Yemen" title="Cabinet of Yemen">Cabinet of Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Political_Council" title="Supreme Political Council">Supreme Political Council</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Transitional_Council" title="Southern Transitional Council">Southern Transitional Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-glorious_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glorious-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> At least 56,000 civilians and combatants have been killed in armed violence in Yemen since January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The war has resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Famine_in_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Famine in Yemen">famine</a> affecting 17 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The lack of safe drinking water, caused by depleted aquifers and the destruction of the country's water infrastructure, has also caused the largest, fastest-spreading <a href="/wiki/Yemen_cholera_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemen cholera outbreak">cholera outbreak</a> in modern history, with the number of suspected cases exceeding 994,751.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup> Over 2,226 people have died since the outbreak began to spread rapidly at the end of April 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The ongoing humanitarian crisis and conflict has received <a href="/wiki/International_reactions_to_the_Saudi-led_intervention_in_Yemen_(2015%E2%80%93present)" title="International reactions to the Saudi-led intervention in Yemen (2015âpresent)">widespread criticism</a> for having a dramatic worsening effect on Yemen's humanitarian situation, that some say has reached the level of a "humanitarian disaster"<sup id="cite_ref-auto_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-31">[31]</a></sup> and some have even labelled it as a <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It has worsened the country's already-poor <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Yemen" title="Human rights in Yemen">human rights record</a>. </p>
      <p>Yemen belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Least_developed_countries" title="Least developed countries">least developed country</a> group,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> referring to its numerous "severe structural impediments to sustainable development".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In 2019, the United Nations reported that Yemen is the country with the most people in need of humanitarian aid, about 24 million people, or 85% of its population.<sup id="cite_ref-ReliefWeb_2019_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReliefWeb_2019-37">[37]</a></sup> As of 2020, the country is placed the highest in <a href="/wiki/Fragile_States_Index" title="Fragile States Index">Fragile State Index</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-fragilestatesindex.org_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fragilestatesindex.org-38">[38]</a></sup> the second worst in <a href="/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index" title="Global Hunger Index">Global Hunger Index</a>, only being surpassed by the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-fragilestatesindex.org_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fragilestatesindex.org-38">[38]</a></sup> and has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> out of all non-African countries. </p>
      <p>It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arabia_Felix" title="Arabia Felix">Arabia Felix</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hamavaran" title="Hamavaran">Hamavaran</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The term <i>Yamnat</i> was mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Arabian" title="Old South Arabian">Old South Arabian</a> inscriptions on the title of one of the kings of the second <a href="/wiki/Himyarite" class="mw-redirect" title="Himyarite">Himyarite</a> kingdom known as Shammar YahrÊ½ish II. The term probably referred to the southwestern coastline of the Arabian peninsula and the southern coastline between <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadramout" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadramout">Hadramout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The historical Yemen included much greater territory than the current nation, stretching from northern <a href="/wiki/%27Asir_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="'Asir Region">'Asir</a> in southwestern Saudi Arabia to <a href="/wiki/Dhofar_Governorate" title="Dhofar Governorate">Dhofar</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One etymology derives Yemen from <i>ymnt</i>, meaning "South", and significantly plays on the notion of the land to the right (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Semitic/yam%C4%ABn-" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Semitic/yamÄ«n-">ð©ºð©£ð©¬</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other sources claim that Yemen is related to <i>yamn</i> or <i>yumn</i>, meaning "felicity" or "blessed", as much of the country is fertile.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The Romans called it <i><a href="/wiki/Arabia_Felix" title="Arabia Felix">Arabia Felix</a></i> ("fertile <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>"), as opposed to <i><a href="/wiki/Arabia_Deserta" title="Arabia Deserta">Arabia Deserta</a></i> ("deserted Arabia"). Latin and <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> writers referred to ancient Yemen as "India", which arose from the Persians calling the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyssinian people">Abyssinians</a> whom they came into contact with in South Arabia by the name of the dark-skinned people who lived next to them, viz. the <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Yemen" title="History of Yemen">History of Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Yemen" title="Ancient history of Yemen">Ancient history of Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qataban" title="Qataban">Qataban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minaeans" title="Minaeans">Minaeans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg/220px-Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg/330px-Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg/440px-Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3833" data-file-height="1835"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jemen1988-022_hg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Marib_Dam" title="Marib Dam">Great Dam of Marib</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>With its long sea border between eastern and western <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a>, Yemen has long existed at a crossroads of cultures with a strategic location in terms of trade on the west of the Arabian Peninsula. Large settlements for their era existed in the mountains of northern Yemen as early as 5000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sabaean" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabaean">Sabaean</a> Kingdom came into existence at least the 11th century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The four major kingdoms or tribal confederations in <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a> were: <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Saba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadramout" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadramout">Hadramout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qataban" title="Qataban">Qataban</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Minaeans" title="Minaeans">Ma'in</a>. <i>Sabaâ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø³ÙÙØ¨ÙÙØ£</span>â)<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran|27|6|e=93|s=ns_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran|27|6|e=93|s=ns-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran|34|15|e=18|s=ns_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran|34|15|e=18|s=ns-51">[51]</a></sup> is thought to be biblical Sheba and was the most prominent federation.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The Sabaean rulers adopted the title <a href="/wiki/Mukarrib" title="Mukarrib">Mukarrib</a> generally thought to mean <i>unifier</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> or a <i>priest-king</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> or the head of the confederation of South Arabian kingdoms, the "king of the kings".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The role of the Mukarrib was to bring the various tribes under the kingdom and preside over them all.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The Sabaeans built the <a href="/wiki/Marib_Dam" title="Marib Dam">Great Dam of Marib</a> around 940 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The dam was built to withstand the seasonal flash floods surging down the valley. </p>
      <p>Between 700 and 680 BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Awsan" title="Kingdom of Awsan">Kingdom of Awsan</a> dominated Aden and its surroundings and challenged the Sabaean supremacy in the Arabian South. Sabaean Mukarrib <a href="/wiki/Karib%27il_Watar" title="Karib'il Watar">Karib'il Watar</a> I conquered the entire realm of Awsan,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and expanded Sabaean rule and territory to include much of <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Lack of water in the Arabian Peninsula prevented the Sabaeans from unifying the entire peninsula. Instead, they established various colonies to control trade routes.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bmane2002-1-114,1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg/220px-Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg/330px-Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg/440px-Bmane2002-1-114%2C1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bmane2002-1-114,1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A funerary <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stela</a> featuring a musical scene, first century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Evidence of Sabaean influence is found in northern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_South_Arabian_script" title="Ancient South Arabian script">South Arabian alphabet</a>, religion and pantheon, and the South Arabian style of art and architecture were introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The Sabaean created a sense of identity through their religion. They worshipped <a href="/wiki/Almaqah" title="Almaqah">El-Maqah</a> and believed that they were his children.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> For centuries, the Sabaeans controlled outbound trade across the <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">strait</a> separating the Arabian Peninsula from the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> from the Indian Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By the third century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Qataban" title="Qataban">Qataban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadramout" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadramout">Hadramout</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Minaeans" title="Minaeans">Ma'in</a> became independent from Saba and established themselves in the Yemeni arena. Minaean rule stretched as far as <a href="/wiki/Dedanites" class="mw-redirect" title="Dedanites">Dedan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> with their capital at <a href="/wiki/Baraqish" title="Baraqish">Baraqish</a>. The Sabaeans regained their control over Ma'in after the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Qataban" title="Qataban">Qataban</a> in 50 BCE. By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Gallus" title="Aelius Gallus">Roman expedition to Arabia Felix</a> in 25 BCE, the Sabaeans were once again the dominating power in Southern Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Gallus" title="Aelius Gallus">Aelius Gallus</a> was ordered to lead a military campaign to establish Roman dominance over the Sabaeans.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> had a vague and contradictory geographical knowledge about Arabia Felix or Yemen. The Roman army of 10,000 men was defeated before <a href="/wiki/Marib" class="mw-redirect" title="Marib">Marib</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>'s close relationship with Aelius Gallus led him to attempt to justify his friend's defeat in his writings. It took the Romans six months to reach Marib and 60 days to return to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The Romans blamed their <a href="/wiki/Nabataeans" title="Nabataeans">Nabataean</a> guide and executed him for treachery.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> No direct mention in Sabaean inscriptions of the Roman expedition has yet been found. </p>
      <p>After the Roman expedition&nbsp;â perhaps earlier&nbsp;â the country fell into chaos, and two clans, namely <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hamdan" title="Banu Hamdan">Hamdan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himyar" class="mw-redirect" title="Himyar">Himyar</a>, claimed kingship, assuming the title King of Sheba and <a href="/wiki/Himyar" class="mw-redirect" title="Himyar">Dhu Raydan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Dhu Raydan, <i>i.e.</i>, Himyarites, allied themselves with <a href="/wiki/Aksum" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksum">Aksum</a> in Ethiopia against the Sabaeans.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The chief of <a href="/wiki/Bakil" title="Bakil">Bakil</a> and king of Saba and Dhu Raydan, <a href="/wiki/Ilasaros" title="Ilasaros">El Sharih Yahdhib</a>, launched successful campaigns against the Himyarites and Habashat, <i>i.e.</i>, <a href="/wiki/Aksum" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksum">Aksum</a>, El Sharih took pride in his campaigns and added the title Yahdhib to his name, which means "suppressor"; he used to kill his enemies by cutting them to pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Sana'a came into prominence during his reign, as he built the <a href="/wiki/Ghumdan_Palace" title="Ghumdan Palace">Ghumdan Palace</a> as his place of residence. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg/170px-Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg/255px-Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg/340px-Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="879"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Dhamar_Ali_Yahbur_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Himyarite" class="mw-redirect" title="Himyarite">Himyarite</a> King Dhamar'ali Yahbur II
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg/170px-British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg/255px-British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg/340px-British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1608" data-file-height="1952"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Yemen_05.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/Sabaean" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabaean">Sabaean</a> gravestone of a woman holding a stylized sheaf of wheat, a symbol of fertility in ancient Yemen
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Himyarite annexed Sana'a from <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hamdan" title="Banu Hamdan">Hamdan</a> around 100 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hashid" title="Hashid">Hashdi</a> tribesmen rebelled against them and regained Sana'a around 180 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shammar_Yahri%27sh" title="Shammar Yahri'sh">Shammar Yahri'sh</a> had not conquered <a href="/wiki/Hadramout" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadramout">Hadramout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Najran" title="Najran">Najran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tihama" class="mw-redirect" title="Tihama">Tihama</a> until 275 CE, thus unifying Yemen and consolidating Himyarite rule.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The Himyarites rejected <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a> and adhered to a consensual form of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> called <a href="/wiki/Rahmanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Rahmanism">Rahmanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 354 CE, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a> sent an embassy headed by <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_the_Indian" title="Theophilos the Indian">Theophilos the Indian</a> to convert the <a href="/wiki/Himyarites" class="mw-redirect" title="Himyarites">Himyarites</a> to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Philostorgius" title="Philostorgius">Philostorgius</a>, the mission was resisted by local Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Shlomo_Sand_p.193_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shlomo_Sand_p.193-80">[80]</a></sup> Several inscriptions have been found in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sabaean_language" title="Sabaean language">Sabaean</a> praising the ruling house in Jewish terms for "...helping and empowering the People of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to Islamic traditions, King <a href="/wiki/Tub%27a_Abu_Kariba_As%27ad" class="mw-redirect" title="Tub'a Abu Kariba As'ad">As'ad the Perfect</a> mounted a military expedition to support the Jews of <a href="/wiki/Yathrib" class="mw-redirect" title="Yathrib">Yathrib</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Abu Kariba As'ad, as known from the inscriptions, led a military campaign to central Arabia or <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> to support the vassal <a href="/wiki/Kindah" title="Kindah">Kingdom of Kindah</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Lakhmids" title="Lakhmids">Lakhmids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> However, no direct reference to Judaism or <a href="/wiki/Yathrib" class="mw-redirect" title="Yathrib">Yathrib</a> was discovered from his lengthy reign. Abu Kariba died in 445 CE, having reigned for almost 50 years.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> By 515 AD, Himyar became increasingly divided along religious lines and a bitter conflict between different factions paved the way for an <a href="/wiki/Aksum" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksum">Aksumite</a> intervention. The last Himyarite king Ma'adikarib Ya'fur was supported by Aksum against his Jewish rivals. Ma'adikarib was Christian and launched a campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Lakhmids" title="Lakhmids">Lakhmids</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, with the support of other Arab allies of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_282_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_282-85">[85]</a></sup> The Lakhmids were a Bulwark of <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>, which was intolerant to a proselytizing religion like Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the death of Ma'adikarib Ya'fur around 521 CE, a Himyarite Jewish <a href="/wiki/Warlord" title="Warlord">warlord</a> named <a href="/wiki/Dhu_Nuwas" title="Dhu Nuwas">Yousef Asar Yathar</a> rose to power with the honorary title of <i>Yathar</i> (meaning, "to avenge"). Yemenite Christians, aided by Aksum and <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, systematically persecuted Jews and burned down several synagogues across the land. Yousef avenged his people with great cruelty.<sup id="cite_ref-Ken_Blady_p.9_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ken_Blady_p.9-87">[87]</a></sup> He marched toward the port city of <a href="/wiki/Mocha,_Yemen" title="Mocha, Yemen">Mocha</a>, killing 14,000 and capturing 11,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_282_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_282-85">[85]</a></sup> Then he settled a camp in <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> to prevent aid flowing from Aksum. At the same time, Yousef sent an army under the command of another Jewish warlord, Sharahil Yaqbul, to <a href="/wiki/Najran" title="Najran">Najran</a>. Sharahil had reinforcements from the Bedouins of the Kindah and <a href="/wiki/Madh%27hij" title="Madh'hij">Madh'hij</a> tribes, eventually wiping out the Christian community in Najran.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Yousef or <a href="/wiki/Dhu_Nuwas" title="Dhu Nuwas">Dhu Nuwas</a> (the one with <a href="/wiki/Payot" title="Payot">sidelocks</a>) as known in Arabic literature, believed that Christians in Yemen were a <a href="/wiki/Fifth_column" title="Fifth column">fifth column</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Christian sources portray Dhu Nuwas (Yousef Asar) as a Jewish zealot, while Islamic traditions say that he threw 20,000 Christians into pits filled with flaming oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Ken_Blady_p.9_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ken_Blady_p.9-87">[87]</a></sup> This history.<sup id="cite_ref-Shlomo_Sand_p.193_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shlomo_Sand_p.193-80">[80]</a></sup> Dhu Nuwas left two inscriptions, neither of them making any reference to fiery pits. Byzantium had to act or lose all credibility as a protector of eastern Christianity. It is reported that Byzantium Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a> sent a letter to the Aksumite <a href="/wiki/Kaleb" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaleb">King Kaleb</a>, pressuring him to "...attack the abominable Hebrew."<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_282_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_282-85">[85]</a></sup> A tripartite military alliance of Byzantine, Aksumite, and Arab Christians successfully defeated Yousef around 525â527 CE and a client Christian king was installed on the Himyarite throne.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Esimiphaios" class="mw-redirect" title="Esimiphaios">Esimiphaios</a> was a local Christian lord, mentioned in an inscription celebrating the burning of an ancient Sabaean palace in <a href="/wiki/Marib" class="mw-redirect" title="Marib">Marib</a> to build a church on its ruins.<sup id="cite_ref-Angelika_Neuwirth_p.49_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angelika_Neuwirth_p.49-91">[91]</a></sup> Three new churches were built in Najran alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Angelika_Neuwirth_p.49_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Angelika_Neuwirth_p.49-91">[91]</a></sup> Many tribes did not recognize Esimiphaios's authority. Esimiphaios was displaced in 531 by a warrior named <a href="/wiki/Abraha" title="Abraha">Abraha</a>, who refused to leave Yemen and declared himself an independent king of Himyar.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_293_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_293-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> sent an embassy to Yemen. He wanted the officially Christian Himyarites to use their influence on the tribes in inner Arabia to launch military operations against <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>. Justinian I bestowed the "dignity of king" upon the Arab <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikhs</a> of Kindah and <a href="/wiki/Ghassanids" title="Ghassanids">Ghassan</a> in central and northern Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_293_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_293-92">[92]</a></sup> From early on, Roman and Byzantine policy was to develop close links with the powers of the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. They were successful in converting<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Aksum and influencing their culture. The results concerning to Yemen were rather disappointing.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Johnson_293_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Johnson_293-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Kindah" title="Kindah">Kendite</a> prince called Yazid bin Kabshat rebelled against <a href="/wiki/Abraha" title="Abraha">Abraha</a> and his Arab Christian allies. A truce was reached once the Great Dam of Marib had suffered a breach.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Abraha died around 570CE; Sources regarding his death are available from the qur'an and hadith. The <a href="/wiki/Sasanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sasanid Empire">Sasanid Empire</a> annexed Aden around 570 CE. Under their rule, most of Yemen enjoyed great autonomy except for Aden and Sana'a. This era marked the collapse of ancient South Arabian civilization since the greater part of the country was under several independent clans until the arrival of Islam in 630 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_history_of_Yemen" title="Islamic history of Yemen">Islamic history of Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advent_of_Islam_and_the_three_dynasties">Advent of Islam and the three dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Advent of Islam and the three dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Yufirids" class="mw-redirect" title="Yufirids">Yufirids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ziyadid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziyadid Dynasty">Ziyadid Dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Imams_of_Yemen" title="Imams of Yemen">Imams of Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg/220px-Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg/440px-Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The interior of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Mosque of Sana'a">Great Mosque of Sana'a</a>, the oldest mosque in Yemen
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> sent his cousin <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> to Sana'a and its surroundings around 630 CE. At the time, Yemen was the most advanced region in Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hamdan" title="Banu Hamdan">Banu Hamdan</a> confederation was among the first to accept Islam, second only to the <a href="/wiki/Somalis" title="Somalis">Somalis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habesha_peoples" title="Habesha peoples">Habesha</a>. Muhammad sent <a href="/wiki/Muadh_ibn_Jabal" title="Muadh ibn Jabal">Muadh ibn Jabal</a>, as well to Al-Janad, in present-day <a href="/wiki/Taiz" title="Taiz">Taiz</a>, and dispatched letters to various tribal leaders. The reason behind this was the division among the tribes and the absence of a strong central authority in Yemen during the days of the prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Major tribes, including Himyar, sent delegations to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> during the "year of delegations" around 630â631 CE. Several Yemenis accepted Islam before the year 630, such as <a href="/wiki/Ammar_ibn_Yasir" title="Ammar ibn Yasir">Ammar ibn Yasir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ala%27a_Al-Hadrami" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ala'a Al-Hadrami">Al-Ala'a Al-Hadrami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miqdad_ibn_Aswad" title="Miqdad ibn Aswad">Miqdad ibn Aswad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa_Ashaari" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Musa Ashaari">Abu Musa Ashaari</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sharhabeel_ibn_Hasana" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharhabeel ibn Hasana">Sharhabeel ibn Hasana</a>. A man named <a href="/wiki/Aswad_Ansi" class="mw-redirect" title="Aswad Ansi">'Abhala ibn Ka'ab Al-Ansi</a> expelled the remaining Persians and claimed he was a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rahman_(Islamic_term)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rahman (Islamic term)">Rahman</a>. He was assassinated by a Yemeni of <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persian</a> origin called <a href="/wiki/Fayruz_al-Daylami" title="Fayruz al-Daylami">Fayruz al-Daylami</a>. Christians, who were mainly staying in <a href="/wiki/Najran" title="Najran">Najran</a> along with Jews, agreed to pay <i><a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">jizyah</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¬ÙÙØ²ÙÙÙÙØ©</span>â), although some Jews converted to Islam, such as <a href="/wiki/Wahb_ibn_Munabbih" title="Wahb ibn Munabbih">Wahb ibn Munabbih</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ka%27ab_al-Ahbar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ka'ab al-Ahbar">Ka'ab al-Ahbar</a>. </p>
      <p>Yemen was stable during the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> Caliphate. Yemeni tribes played a pivotal role in the Islamic expansion of Egypt, Iraq, Persia, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_Southern_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Islam in Southern Italy">Sicily</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Yemeni tribes who settled in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, contributed significantly to the solidification of <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> rule, especially during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Marwan_I" title="Marwan I">Marwan I</a>. Powerful Yemenite tribes such as Kindah were on his side during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marj_Rahit_(684)" title="Battle of Marj Rahit (684)">Battle of Marj Rahit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Several emirates led by people of Yemeni descent were established in North Africa and <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>. Effective control over entire Yemen was not achieved by the Umayyad Caliphate. <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> Abdullah ibn Yahya Al-Kindi was elected in 745 CE to lead <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibadi">the IbÄá¸Ä« movement</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hadramawt" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadramawt">Hadramawt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>. He expelled the Umayyad governor from Sana'a and captured <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and Medina in 746.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated237_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated237-102">[102]</a></sup> Al-Kindi, known by his nickname "Talib al-Haqq" (seeker of truth), established the first <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a> state in the history of Islam but was killed in Taif around 749.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated237_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated237-102">[102]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Ziyad founded the <a href="/wiki/Ziyadid_dynasty" title="Ziyadid dynasty">Ziyadid dynasty</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tihama" class="mw-redirect" title="Tihama">Tihama</a> around 818 CE. The state stretched from <a href="/wiki/Al_Qunfudhah" title="Al Qunfudhah">Haly</a> (in present-day Saudi Arabia) to Aden. They nominally recognized the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> but were ruling independently from their capital in <a href="/wiki/Zabid" title="Zabid">Zabid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated128_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated128-103">[103]</a></sup> The history of this dynasty is obscure. They never exercised control over the highlands and Hadramawt, and did not control more than a coastal strip of Yemen (Tihama) bordering the Red Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> A Himyarite clan called the <a href="/wiki/Yufirids" class="mw-redirect" title="Yufirids">Yufirids</a> established their rule over the highlands from Saada to Taiz, while Hadramawt was an <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a> stronghold and rejected all allegiance to the Abbasids in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated128_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated128-103">[103]</a></sup> By virtue of its location, the <a href="/wiki/Ziyadid_dynasty" title="Ziyadid dynasty">Ziyadid dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zabid" title="Zabid">Zabid</a> developed a special relationship with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Abyssinia</a>. The chief of the <a href="/wiki/Dahlak_Archipelago" title="Dahlak Archipelago">Dahlak</a> islands exported slaves, as well as amber and leopard hides, to the then ruler of Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-Lunpor_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lunpor-105">[105]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidi</a> imam, <a href="/wiki/Al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq_Yahya" title="Al-Hadi ila'l-Haqq Yahya">Yahya ibn al-Husayn</a>, arrived in Yemen in 893 CE. He was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Rassids" class="mw-redirect" title="Rassids">Zaidi imamate</a> in 897. He was a religious cleric and judge who was invited to come to Saada from Medina to arbitrate tribal disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Imam Yahya persuaded local tribesmen to follow his teachings. The sect slowly spread across the highlands, as the tribes of <a href="/wiki/Hashid" title="Hashid">Hashid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bakil" title="Bakil">Bakil</a>, later known as "the twin wings of the imamate," accepted his authority.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq_Yahya" title="Al-Hadi ila'l-Haqq Yahya">Yahya</a> established his influence in Saada and Najran. He also tried to capture Sana'a from the Yufirids in 901 CE but failed miserably. In 904, the <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ili" class="mw-redirect" title="Isma'ili">Isma'ilis</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hawshab" title="Ibn Hawshab">Ibn Hawshab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Fadl_al-Jayshani" title="Ali ibn al-Fadl al-Jayshani">Ali ibn al-Fadl al-Jayshani</a> invaded Sana'a. The Yufirid emir As'ad ibn Ibrahim retreated to <a href="/wiki/Al_Jawf_Governorate" title="Al Jawf Governorate">Al-Jawf</a>, and between 904 and 913, Sana'a was conquered no less than 20 times by Isma'ilis and Yufirids.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> As'ad ibn Ibrahim regained Sana'a in 915. Yemen was in turmoil as Sana'a became a battlefield for the three dynasties, as well as independent tribes. </p>
      <p>The Yufirid emir Abdullah ibn Qahtan attacked and burned Zabid in 989, severely weakening the <a href="/wiki/Ziyadid_dynasty" title="Ziyadid dynasty">Ziyadid dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The Ziyadid monarchs lost effective power after 989, or even earlier than that. Meanwhile, a succession of slaves held power in Zabid and continued to govern in the name of their masters, eventually establishing their own <a href="/wiki/Najahid_dynasty" title="Najahid dynasty">dynasty</a> around 1022 or 1050 according to different sources.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Although they were recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> in Baghdad, they ruled no more than Zabid and four districts to its north.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The rise of the Isma'ili <a href="/wiki/Sulayhid_dynasty" title="Sulayhid dynasty">Sulayhid dynasty</a> in the Yemeni highlands reduced their history to a series of intrigues. </p> 
      <h4><span id="Sulayhid_Dynasty_.281047.E2.80.931138.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulayhid_Dynasty_(1047â1138)">Sulayhid Dynasty (1047â1138)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sulayhid Dynasty (1047â1138)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sulayhid_dynasty" title="Sulayhid dynasty">Sulayhid dynasty</a>
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG/200px-Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG/300px-Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG/400px-Jibla_IMG_5662.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Jibla,_Yemen" title="Jibla, Yemen">Jibla</a> became the capital of the dynasty. Featured is the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Arwa_Mosque" title="Queen Arwa Mosque">Queen Arwa Mosque</a>.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg/200px-Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg/300px-Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg/400px-Queen_Arwa_al-_Sulaihi_Palace_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Queen_Arwa" title="Palace of Queen Arwa">Queen Arwa al-Sulaihi Palace</a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sulayhid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulayhid">Sulayhid</a> dynasty was founded in the northern highlands around 1040; at the time, Yemen was ruled by different local dynasties. In 1060, <a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Sulayhi" title="Ali al-Sulayhi">Ali ibn Muhammad Al-Sulayhi</a> conquered Zabid and killed its ruler Al-Najah, founder of the Najahid dynasty. His sons were forced to flee to <a href="/wiki/Dahlak_Archipelago" title="Dahlak Archipelago">Dahlak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Hadramawt fell into Sulayhid hands after their capture of Aden in 1162.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 1063, Ali had subjugated <a href="/wiki/Greater_Yemen" title="Greater Yemen">Greater Yemen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> He then marched toward <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> and occupied <a href="/wiki/Makkah" class="mw-redirect" title="Makkah">Makkah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Ali was married to <a href="/wiki/Asma_bint_Shihab" title="Asma bint Shihab">Asma bint Shihab</a>, who governed Yemen with her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated14_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated14-116">[116]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Khutba" class="mw-redirect" title="Khutba">Khutba</a> during <a href="/wiki/Jumu%27ah" title="Jumu'ah">Friday prayers</a> was proclaimed in both her husband's name and hers. No other Arab woman had this honor since the advent of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated14_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated14-116">[116]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Sulayhi" title="Ali al-Sulayhi">Ali al-Sulayhi</a> was killed by Najah's sons on his way to Mecca in 1084. His son Ahmed Al-Mukarram led an army to Zabid and killed 8,000 of its inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> He later installed the Zurayids to govern Aden. al-Mukarram, who had been afflicted with facial paralysis resulting from war injuries, retired in 1087 and handed over power to his wife <a href="/wiki/Arwa_al-Sulayhi" title="Arwa al-Sulayhi">Arwa al-Sulayhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Queen Arwa moved the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Sulayhid_dynasty" title="Sulayhid dynasty">Sulayhid dynasty</a> from Sana'a to <a href="/wiki/Jibla,_Yemen" title="Jibla, Yemen">Jibla</a>, a small town in central Yemen near <a href="/wiki/Ibb" title="Ibb">Ibb</a>. Jibla was strategically near the Sulayhid dynasty source of wealth, the agricultural central highlands. It was also within easy reach of the southern portion of the country, especially Aden. She sent Ismaili missionaries to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, where a significant Ismaili community was formed that exists to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated51_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated51-119">[119]</a></sup> Queen Arwa continued to rule securely until her death in 1138.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated51_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated51-119">[119]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Arwa al-Sulayhi is still remembered as a great and much-loved sovereign, as attested in Yemeni historiography, literature, and popular lore, where she is referred to as <i>Balqis al-sughra</i> ("the junior queen of Sheba").<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Although the Sulayhids were Ismaili, they never tried to impose their beliefs on the public.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Shortly after Queen Arwa's death, the country was split between five competing petty dynasties along religious lines.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid dynasty</a> overthrew the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> in Egypt. A few years after their rise to power, <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a> dispatched his brother <a href="/wiki/Turan_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Turan Shah">Turan Shah</a> to conquer Yemen in 1174.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span id="Ayyubid_conquest_.281171.E2.80.931260.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ayyubid_conquest_(1171â1260)">Ayyubid conquest (1171â1260)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ayyubid conquest (1171â1260)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayyubid Dynasty">Ayyubid Dynasty</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Turan_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Turan Shah">Turan Shah</a> conquered Zabid from the <a href="/wiki/Mahdids" title="Mahdids">Mahdids</a> in May 1174, then marched toward Aden in June and captured it from the Zurayids.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hamdanid_sultans" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamdanid sultans">Hamdanid sultans</a> of Sana'a resisted the Ayyubid in 1175, and the Ayyubids did not manage to secure Sana'a until 1189.<sup id="cite_ref-Farhad_Daftary_2007_260_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farhad_Daftary_2007_260-125">[125]</a></sup> The Ayyubid rule was stable in southern and central Yemen, where they succeeded in eliminating the ministates of that region, while Ismaili and Zaidi tribesmen continued to hold out in several fortresses.<sup id="cite_ref-Farhad_Daftary_2007_260_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farhad_Daftary_2007_260-125">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Ayyubids failed to capture the Zaydis stronghold in northern Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> In 1191, Zaydis of <a href="/wiki/Shibam_Kawkaban_District" title="Shibam Kawkaban District">Shibam Kawkaban</a> rebelled and killed 700 Ayyubid soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur_Abdallah" title="Al-Mansur Abdallah">Abdullah bin Hamza</a> proclaimed the imamate in 1197 and fought al-Mu'izz Ismail, the Ayyubid Sultan of Yemen. Imam Abdullah was defeated at first but was able to conquer Sana'a and <a href="/wiki/Dhamar,_Yemen" title="Dhamar, Yemen">Dhamar</a> in 1198,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and al-Mu'izz Ismail was assassinated in 1202.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur_Abdallah" title="Al-Mansur Abdallah">Abdullah bin Hamza</a> carried on the struggle against the Ayyubid until his death in 1217. After his demise, the Zaidi community was split between two rival imams. The Zaydis were dispersed and a truce was signed with the Ayyubid in 1219.<sup id="cite_ref-Mohammed_Abdo_Al-Sururi_1987_407_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammed_Abdo_Al-Sururi_1987_407-130">[130]</a></sup> The Ayyubid army was defeated in <a href="/wiki/Dhamar,_Yemen" title="Dhamar, Yemen">Dhamar</a> in 1226.<sup id="cite_ref-Mohammed_Abdo_Al-Sururi_1987_407_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohammed_Abdo_Al-Sururi_1987_407-130">[130]</a></sup> Ayyubid Sultan Mas'ud Yusuf left for Mecca in 1228, never to return.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230-131">[131]</a></sup> Other sources suggest that he was forced to leave for <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> instead in 1123.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_84_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_84-132">[132]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span id="Rasulid_Dynasty_.281229.E2.80.931454.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rasulid_Dynasty_(1229â1454)">Rasulid Dynasty (1229â1454)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rasulid Dynasty (1229â1454)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_dynasty" title="Rasulid dynasty">Rasulid dynasty</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz,Yemen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg/220px-Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg/330px-Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg/440px-Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz%2CYemen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="609"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_Castle_GardenTaiz,Yemen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Cairo_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Cairo Castle">Al-Qahyra (Cairo) Castle's</a> Garden in Taiz, the capital of Yemen during the <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_dynasty" title="Rasulid dynasty">Rasulid's era</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Rasulid Dynasty">Rasulid Dynasty</a> was established in 1229 by Umar ibn Rasul, who was appointed deputy governor by the Ayyubids in 1223. When the last Ayyubid ruler left Yemen in 1229, Umar stayed in the country as caretaker. He subsequently declared himself an independent king by assuming the title "al-Malik Al-Mansur" (the king assisted by <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_84_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_84-132">[132]</a></sup> Umar established the Rasulid dynasty on a firm foundation and expanded its territory to include the area from <a href="/wiki/Dhofar" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhofar">Dhofar</a> to Mecca<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_854_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_854-133">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Umar first established himself at Zabid, then moved into the mountainous interior, taking the important highland centre Sana'a. However, the Rasulid capitals were Zabid and Taiz. He was assassinated by his nephew in 1249.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230-131">[131]</a></sup> Omar's son Yousef defeated the faction led by his father's assassins and crushed several counter-attacks by the Zaydi imams who still held on in the northern highland. Mainly because of the victories he scored over his rivals, he assumed the honorific title "al-Muzaffar" (the victorious).<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_86_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_86-134">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p> After the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">fall of Baghdad</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> in 1258, al-Muzaffar Yusuf I appropriated the title of <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_86_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_86-134">[134]</a></sup> He chose the city of Taiz to become the political capital of the kingdom because of its strategic location and proximity to Aden.<sup id="cite_ref-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669-135">[135]</a></sup> al-Muzaffar Yusuf I died in 1296, having reigned for 47 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_86_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_86-134">[134]</a></sup> When the news of his death reached the Zaydi imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_al-Mutahhar_bin_Yahya" title="Al-Mutawakkil al-Mutahhar bin Yahya">Al-Mutawakkil al-Mutahhar bin Yahya</a>, he commented,<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_86_134-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_86-134">[134]</a></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>The greatest king of Yemen, the <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah</a> of the time, has died. His pens used to break our lances and swords to pieces.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/220px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/330px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/440px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="829" data-file-height="910"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 13th-century slave market in Yemen
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Rasulid state nurtured Yemen's commercial links with India and the Far East.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> They profited greatly by the Red Sea transit trade via Aden and Zabid.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230-131">[131]</a></sup> The economy also boomed due to the agricultural development programs instituted by the kings who promoted massive cultivation of palms.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230_131-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230-131">[131]</a></sup> The Rasulid kings enjoyed the support of the population of <a href="/wiki/Tihama" class="mw-redirect" title="Tihama">Tihama</a> and southern Yemen, while they had to buy the loyalty of Yemen's restive northern highland tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230_131-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_230-131">[131]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Rasulid sultans built numerous <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">Madrasas</a> to solidify the <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a> school of thought, which is still the dominant school of <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">jurisprudence</a> amongst Yemenis today.<sup id="cite_ref-David_J_Wasserstein,_Ami_Ayalon_2013_201_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_J_Wasserstein,_Ami_Ayalon_2013_201-137">[137]</a></sup> Under their rule, Taiz and Zabid became major international centres of Islamic learning.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_231_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_231-138">[138]</a></sup> The kings themselves were educated men in their own right, who not only had important libraries but also wrote treatises on a wide array of subjects, ranging from astrology and medicine to agriculture and genealogy.<sup id="cite_ref-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669-135">[135]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The dynasty is regarded as the greatest native Yemeni state since the fall of the pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> They were of <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">Turkic</a> descent.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> They claimed an ancient Yemenite origin to justify their rule. The Rasulids were not the first dynasty to create a fictitious genealogy for political purposes, nor were they doing anything out of the ordinary in the tribal context of Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1993_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1993-141">[141]</a></sup> By claiming descent from a solid Yemenite tribe, the Rasulids brought Yemen to a vital sense of unity in an otherwise chaotic regional milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1993_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1993-141">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>They had a difficult relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Mamluks_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluks of Egypt">Mamluks of Egypt</a> because the latter considered them a vassal state.<sup id="cite_ref-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669-135">[135]</a></sup> Their competition centred over the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> and the right to provide <i><a href="/wiki/Kiswa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kiswa">kiswa</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Ka%27aba" class="mw-redirect" title="Ka'aba">Ka'aba</a> in Mecca.<sup id="cite_ref-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josef_W._Meri,_Jere_L._Bacharach_2006_669-135">[135]</a></sup> The dynasty became increasingly threatened by disgruntled family members over the problem of succession, combined by periodic tribal revolts, as they were locked in a war of attrition with the Zaydi imams in the northern highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_231_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_D._Knysh_1999_231-138">[138]</a></sup> During the last 12 years of Rasulid rule, the country was torn between several contenders for the kingdom. The weakening of the Rasulid provided an opportunity for the <a href="/wiki/Tahirids_(Yemen)" title="Tahirids (Yemen)">Banu Taher</a> clan to take over and establish themselves as the new rulers of Yemen in 1454 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-David_J_Wasserstein,_Ami_Ayalon_2013_201_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_J_Wasserstein,_Ami_Ayalon_2013_201-137">[137]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span id="Tahiride_Dynasty_.281454.E2.80.931517.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tahiride_Dynasty_(1454â1517)">Tahiride Dynasty (1454â1517)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tahiride Dynasty (1454â1517)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tahirids_(Yemen)" title="Tahirids (Yemen)">Tahirids (Yemen)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Assault_on_Aden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Assault_on_Aden.jpg/220px-Assault_on_Aden.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Assault_on_Aden.jpg/330px-Assault_on_Aden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Assault_on_Aden.jpg/440px-Assault_on_Aden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="808" data-file-height="463"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Assault_on_Aden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portuguese Viceroy <a href="/wiki/Afonso_de_Albuquerque" title="Afonso de Albuquerque">Afonso de Albuquerque</a> failed twice to <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Aden" title="Siege of Aden">conquer Aden</a>, though the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> managed to rule <a href="/wiki/Socotra" title="Socotra">Socotra</a> until 1511.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Tahirids_(Yemen)" title="Tahirids (Yemen)">Tahirids</a> were a local clan based in <a href="/wiki/Rada%27_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Rada' District">Rada'a</a>. While they were not as impressive as their predecessors, they were still keen builders. They built schools, mosques, and irrigation channels, as well as water cisterns and bridges in Zabid, Aden, <a href="/wiki/Rada%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Rada'a">Rada'a</a>, and Juban. Their best-known monument is the <a href="/wiki/Amiriya_Madrasa" title="Amiriya Madrasa">Amiriya Madrasa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rada%27_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Rada' District">Rada' District</a>, which was built in 1504. </p>
      <p>The Tahiride were too weak either to contain the <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaydi imams</a> or to defend themselves against foreign attacks. </p>
      <p>Realizing how rich the Tahiride realm was, they decided to conquer it.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated59_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated59-142">[142]</a></sup> The Mamluk army, with the support of forces loyal to Zaydi Imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_Yahya_Sharaf_ad-Din" title="Al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din">Al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din</a>, conquered the entire realm of the Tahiride but failed to capture Aden in 1517. The Mamluk victory was short-lived. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> conquered Egypt, hanging the last Mamluk Sultan in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated59_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated59-142">[142]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ottomans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> had not decided to conquer Yemen until 1538.
