The Zaydi highland tribes emerged as national heroes<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_94_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_94-143">[143]</a></sup> by offering stiff, vigorous resistance to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Turkish</a> occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-144">[144]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mamluks_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluks of Egypt">Mamluks of Egypt</a> tried to attach Yemen to Egypt and the Portuguese led by <a href="/wiki/Afonso_de_Albuquerque" title="Afonso de Albuquerque">Afonso de Albuquerque</a>, occupied <a href="/wiki/Socotra" title="Socotra">Socotra</a> and made an unsuccessful attack on Aden in 1513.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Modern_history_of_Yemen" title="Modern history of Yemen">Modern history of Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Zaydis_and_Ottomans">The Zaydis and Ottomans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Zaydis and Ottomans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Eyalet" title="Yemen Eyalet">Yemen Eyalet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Zaidi_State" title="Yemeni Zaidi State">Yemeni Zaidi State</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg/220px-Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg/330px-Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg/440px-Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4061" data-file-height="2562"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jemen1988-153_hg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Al-Bakiriyya_Mosque" title="Al-Bakiriyya Mosque">Al Bakiriyya Ottoman Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sana%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Sana'a">Sana'a</a>, was built in 1597
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif/lossless-page1-220px-Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif/lossless-page1-330px-Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif/lossless-page1-440px-Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif.png 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="2532"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Group_of_men_in_Yemen.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ottoman soldiers and Yemeni locals
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p> The Ottomans had two fundamental interests to safeguard in Yemen: The Islamic holy cities of Mecca and Medina, and the trade route with India in spices and textilesâboth threatened, and the latter virtually eclipsed, by the arrival of the Portuguese in the Indian Ocean and the Red Sea in the early 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Had%C4%B1m_Suleiman_Pasha" title="HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha">HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha</a>, The Ottoman governor of <a href="/wiki/Eyalet_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyalet of Egypt">Egypt</a>, was ordered to command a fleet of 90 ships to conquer Yemen. The country was in a state of incessant anarchy and discord as <a href="/wiki/Had%C4%B1m_Suleiman_Pasha" title="HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha">HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha</a> described it by saying:<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"></p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Yemen is a land with no lord, an empty province. It would be not only possible but easy to capture, and should it be captured, it would be master of the lands of <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a> and send every year a great amount of gold and jewels to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.</p>
      </blockquote>
      <p> Imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_Yahya_Sharaf_ad-Din" title="Al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din">al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din</a> ruled over the northern highlands including Sana'a, while Aden was held by the last Tahiride Sultan 'Amir ibn Dauod. <a href="/wiki/Had%C4%B1m_Suleiman_Pasha" title="HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha">HadÄ±m Suleiman Pasha</a> stormed Aden in 1538, killing its ruler, and extended Ottoman authority to include Zabid in 1539 and eventually Tihama in its entirety.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«88_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«88-148">[148]</a></sup> Zabid became the administrative headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Eyalet" title="Yemen Eyalet">Yemen Eyalet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«88_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«88-148">[148]</a></sup> The Ottoman governors did not exercise much control over the highlands. They held sway mainly in the southern coastal region, particularly around Zabid, <a href="/wiki/Mocha,_Yemen" title="Mocha, Yemen">Mocha</a>, and Aden.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Of 80,000 soldiers sent to Yemen from Egypt between 1539 and 1547, only 7,000 survived.<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_W._Stookey_1978_134_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_W._Stookey_1978_134-150">[150]</a></sup> The Ottoman accountant-general in Egypt remarked:<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_W._Stookey_1978_134_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_W._Stookey_1978_134-150">[150]</a></sup>
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"></p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>We have seen no foundry like Yemen for our soldiers. Each time we have sent an expeditionary force there, it has melted away like salt dissolved in water.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottomans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> sent yet another expeditionary force to Zabid in 1547, while Imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_Yahya_Sharaf_ad-Din" title="Al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din">al-Mutawakkil Yahya Sharaf ad-Din</a> was ruling the highlands independently. Imam al-Mutawakkil Yahya chose his son Ali to succeed him, a decision that infuriated his other son <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutahhar" title="Al-Mutahhar">al-Mutahhar</a> ibn Yahya.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«95_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«95-151">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutahhar" title="Al-Mutahhar">Al-Mutahhar</a> was lame, so he was not qualified for the imamate.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«95_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«95-151">[151]</a></sup> He urged Oais Pasha, the Ottoman colonial governor in <a href="/wiki/Zabid" title="Zabid">Zabid</a>, to attack his father.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Indeed, Ottoman troops supported by tribal forces loyal to Imam al-Mutahhar stormed Taiz and marched north toward Sana'a in August 1547. The Turks officially made Imam al-Mutahhar a <i><a href="/wiki/Sanjak-bey" title="Sanjak-bey">Sanjak-bey</a></i> with authority over <a href="/wiki/%27Amran" title="'Amran">'Amran</a>. Imam al-Mutahhar assassinated the Ottoman colonial governor and recaptured Sana'a, but the Ottomans, led by <a href="/wiki/%C3%96zdemir_Pasha" title="Ãzdemir Pasha">Ãzdemir Pasha</a>, forced al-Mutahhar to retreat to his fortress in <a href="/wiki/Thula" title="Thula">Thula</a>. Ãzdemir Pasha effectively put Yemen under Ottoman rule between 1552 and 1560. He was considered a competent ruler given Yemen's notorious lawlessness, garrisoning the main cities, building new fortresses, and rendering secure the main routes.<sup id="cite_ref-Halil_Ä°nalcÄ±k,_Donald_Quataert_19894_333_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halil_Ä°nalcÄ±k,_Donald_Quataert_19894_333-153">[153]</a></sup> Ãzdemir died in Sana'a in 1561 and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Pasha_(governor)" title="Mahmud Pasha (governor)">Mahmud Pasha</a>. </p>
      <p>Unlike Ãzdemir's brief but able leadership, Mahmud Pasha was described by other Ottoman officials as a corrupt and unscrupulous governor. He used his authority to take over several castles, some of which belonged to the former <a href="/wiki/Rasulid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Rasulid Dynasty">Rasulid kings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«95_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«95-151">[151]</a></sup> Mahmud Pasha killed a <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> scholar from <a href="/wiki/Ibb" title="Ibb">Ibb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«132_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«132-154">[154]</a></sup> The Ottoman historian claimed that this incident was celebrated by the <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaydi Shia</a> community in the northern highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«132_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«132-154">[154]</a></sup> Disregarding the delicate balance of power in Yemen by acting tactlessly, he alienated different groups within Yemeni society, causing them to forget their rivalries and unite against the Turks.<sup id="cite_ref-Halil_Ä°nalcÄ±k,_Donald_Quataert_19894_333_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halil_Ä°nalcÄ±k,_Donald_Quataert_19894_333-153">[153]</a></sup> Mahmud Pasha was displaced by Ridvan Pasha in 1564. By 1565, Yemen was split into two provinces, the highlands under the command of Ridvan Pasha and Tihama under Murad Pasha. Imam al-Mutahhar launched a propaganda campaign in which he claimed that the prophet Mohammed came to him in a dream and advised him to wage <i>jihad</i> against the Ottomans.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Al-Mutahhar led the tribes to capture Sana'a from Ridvan Pasha in 1567. When Murad tried to relieve Sana'a, highland tribesmen ambushed his unit and slaughtered all of them.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«180_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«180-156">[156]</a></sup> Over 80 battles were fought. The last decisive encounter took place in Dhamar around 1568, in which Murad Pasha was beheaded and his head sent to al-Mutahhar in Sana'a.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«180_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«180-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_103_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_103-157">[157]</a></sup> By 1568, only Zabid remained under the possession of the Turks.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_103_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_103-157">[157]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:612px;">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Thula_fortification2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Thula_fortification2.jpg/610px-Thula_fortification2.jpg" decoding="async" width="610" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Thula_fortification2.jpg/915px-Thula_fortification2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Thula_fortification2.jpg/1220px-Thula_fortification2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1589" data-file-height="604"></a> 
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Thula_fortification2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
          </div>
          <div style="text-align: center;">
            Ruins of Thula fortress in <a href="/wiki/%27Amran" title="'Amran">'Amran</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutahhar" title="Al-Mutahhar">al-Mutahhar</a> ibn Yahya barricaded himself against Ottoman attacks
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Lala_Kara_Mustafa_Pasha" class="mw-redirect" title="Lala Kara Mustafa Pasha">Lala Kara Mustafa Pasha</a>, the Ottoman governor of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Syria" title="Ottoman Syria">Syria</a>, was ordered by <a href="/wiki/Selim_II" title="Selim II">Selim II</a> to suppress the Yemeni rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«198_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«198-158">[158]</a></sup> However, the Turkish army in Egypt was reluctant to go to Yemen due to their knowledge of the hegemony of the northern Yemenis.<sup id="cite_ref-NahrawÄlÄ«198_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NahrawÄlÄ«198-158">[158]</a></sup> Mustafa Pasha sent a letter with two Turkish <i><a href="/wiki/Sergeant" title="Sergeant">shawishes</a></i> hoping to persuade al-Mutahhar to give an apology and confirm that Mustafa Pasha did not promote any act of aggression against the Ottoman army, and state that the "ignorant Arabian" according to the Turks, acted on their own.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Imam al-Mutahhar refused the Ottoman offer. When Mustafa Pasha sent an expeditionary force under the command of Uthman Pasha, it was defeated with great casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Sultan Selim II was infuriated by Mustafa's hesitation to go to Yemen. He executed a number of <i>sanjak-beys</i> in Egypt and ordered <a href="/wiki/Sinan_Pasha" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinan Pasha">Sinan Pasha</a> to lead the entire Turkish army in Egypt to reconquer Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Sinan Pasha was a prominent Ottoman general of <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanian</a> origin.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_103_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_103-157">[157]</a></sup> He reconquered Aden, Taiz, and Ibb, and besieged <a href="/wiki/Shibam_Kawkaban_District" title="Shibam Kawkaban District">Shibam Kawkaban</a> in 1570 for seven months. The siege was lifted once a truce was reached.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Imam al-Mutahhar was pushed back, but could not be entirely overcome.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> After al-Mutahhar's demise in 1572, the Zaydi community was not united under an imam; the Turks took advantage of their disunity and conquered Sana'a, <a href="/wiki/Sa%27dah" title="Sa'dah">Sa'dah</a>, and Najran in 1583.<sup id="cite_ref-Michel_Tuchscherer_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_Tuchscherer-164">[164]</a></sup> Imam <a href="/wiki/An-Nasir_al-Hasan_bin_Ali" title="An-Nasir al-Hasan bin Ali">al-Nasir Hassan</a> was arrested in 1585 and exiled to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, thereby putting an end to the Yemeni rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdul_Ali_1996_103_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdul_Ali_1996_103-157">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Zaydi tribesmen in the northern highlands particularly those of <a href="/wiki/Hashid" title="Hashid">Hashid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bakil" title="Bakil">Bakil</a>, were ever the Turkish bugbear in all Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The Ottomans who justified their presence in Yemen as a triumph for Islam, accused the Zaydis of being <a href="/wiki/Kafir" title="Kafir">infidels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Hassan Pasha was appointed governor of Yemen and enjoyed a period of relative peace from 1585 to 1597. Pupils of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur_al-Qasim" title="Al-Mansur al-Qasim">al-Mansur al-Qasim</a> suggested he should claim the imamate and fight the Turks. He declined at first, but the promotion of the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">jurisprudence</a> at the expense of Zaydi Islam infuriated al-Mansur al-Qasim. He proclaimed the imamate in September 1597, which was the same year the Ottoman authorities inaugurated <a href="/wiki/Al-Bakiriyya_Mosque" title="Al-Bakiriyya Mosque">al-Bakiriyya Mosque</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Michel_Tuchscherer_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_Tuchscherer-164">[164]</a></sup> By 1608, Imam al-Mansur (the victorious) regained control over the highlands and signed a truce for 10 years with the Ottomans.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Imam al-Mansur al-Qasim died in 1620. His son <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27ayyad_Muhammad" title="Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad">Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad</a> succeeded him and confirmed the truce with the Ottomans. In 1627, the Ottomans lost Aden and <a href="/wiki/Lahej" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahej">Lahej</a>. 'Abdin Pasha was ordered to suppress the rebels, but failed, and had to retreat to Mocha.<sup id="cite_ref-Michel_Tuchscherer_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_Tuchscherer-164">[164]</a></sup> Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad expelled the Ottomans from Sana'a in 1628, only Zabid and Mocha remained under Ottoman possession. Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad captured Zabid in 1634 and allowed the Ottomans to leave Mocha peacefully.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The reason behind Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad's success was the possession of firearms by the tribes and their unity behind him.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mocha1692.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Mocha1692.jpg/220px-Mocha1692.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Mocha1692.jpg/330px-Mocha1692.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Mocha1692.jpg/440px-Mocha1692.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1874" data-file-height="1238"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mocha1692.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mocha,_Yemen" title="Mocha, Yemen">Mocha</a> was Yemen's busiest port in the 17th and 18th centuries
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1632, Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad sent an expeditionary force of 1,000 men to conquer Mecca.<sup id="cite_ref-Faulder_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faulder-170">[170]</a></sup> The army entered the city in triumph and killed its governor.<sup id="cite_ref-Faulder_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faulder-170">[170]</a></sup> The Ottomans were not ready to lose Mecca after Yemen, so they sent an army from Egypt to fight the Yemenites.<sup id="cite_ref-Faulder_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faulder-170">[170]</a></sup> Seeing that the Turkish army was too numerous to overcome, the Yemeni army retreated to a valley outside Mecca.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Faulder_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Faulder-171">[171]</a></sup> Ottoman troops attacked the Yemenis by hiding at the wells that supplied them with water. This plan proceeded successfully, causing the Yemenis over 200 casualties, most from thirst.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Faulder_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Faulder-171">[171]</a></sup> The tribesmen eventually surrendered and returned to Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Al-Mu'ayyad Muhammad died in 1644. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_Isma%27il" title="Al-Mutawakkil Isma'il">Al-Mutawakkil Isma'il</a>, another son of al-Mansur al-Qasim, who conquered Yemen in its entirety, from <a href="/wiki/Asir" class="mw-redirect" title="Asir">Asir</a> in the north to Dhofar in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> During his reign, and during the reign of his successor, <a href="/wiki/Al-Mahdi_Ahmad" title="Al-Mahdi Ahmad">Al-Mahdi Ahmad</a> (1676â1681), the imamate implemented some of the harshest discriminatory laws (<i>ghiyar</i>) against the Jews of Yemen, which culminated in the expulsion of all Jews (<a href="/wiki/Exile_of_Mawza" class="mw-redirect" title="Exile of Mawza">Exile of Mawza</a>) to a hot and arid region in the Tihama coastal plain. The Qasimid state was the strongest Zaydi state to ever exist. See <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Zaidi_State" title="Yemeni Zaidi State">Yemeni Zaidi State</a> for more information. </p>
      <p>During that period, Yemen was the sole <a href="/wiki/Coffee_bean" title="Coffee bean">coffee</a> producer in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The country established diplomatic relations with the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> of Persia, Ottomans of Hejaz, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> in India, and Ethiopia, as well. <a href="/wiki/Fasilides" title="Fasilides">Fasilides</a> of Ethiopia sent three diplomatic missions to Yemen, but the relations did not develop into a political alliance, as Fasilides had hoped, due to the rise of powerful feudalists in his country.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> In the first half of the 18th century, the Europeans broke Yemen's monopoly on coffee by smuggling coffee trees and cultivating them in their own colonies in the East Indies, East Africa, the West Indies, and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The imamate did not follow a cohesive mechanism for succession, and family quarrels and tribal insubordination led to the political decline of the Qasimi dynasty in the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> In 1728 or 1731, the chief representative of <a href="/wiki/Lahej" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahej">Lahej</a> declared himself an independent sultan in defiance of the Qasimid dynasty and conquered Aden, thus establishing the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Lahej" title="Sultanate of Lahej">Sultanate of Lahej</a>. The rising power of the fervently Islamist <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> movement on the Arabian Peninsula cost the Zaidi state its coastal possessions after 1803. The imam was able to regain them temporarily in 1818, but new intervention by the Ottoman viceroy of Egypt in 1833 again wrested the coast from the ruler in Sana'a. After 1835, the imamate changed hands with great frequency and some imams were assassinated. After 1849, the Zaidi polity descended into chaos that lasted for decades.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Britain_and_the_Nine_Regions">Great Britain and the Nine Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Great Britain and the Nine Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Aden_Protectorate" title="Aden Protectorate">Aden Protectorate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Lahej" title="Sultanate of Lahej">Sultanate of Lahej</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Yem5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Yem5.jpg/170px-Yem5.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Yem5.jpg/255px-Yem5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Yem5.jpg/340px-Yem5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="743" data-file-height="704"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Yem5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The building of the Legislative Council of Aden, built by the English in the 19th century as St. Mary's Church, was converted into the building of the Legislative Council in the 1960s, and is now a museum
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The British were looking for a coal depot to service their steamers en route to India. It took 700 tons of coal for a round-trip from <a href="/wiki/Suez" title="Suez">Suez</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a>. <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> officials decided on Aden. The <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> tried to reach an agreement with the Zaydi imam of Sana'a, permitting them a foothold in Mocha, and when unable to secure their position, they extracted a similar agreement from the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Lahej" title="Sultanate of Lahej">Sultan of Lahej</a>, enabling them to consolidate a position in Aden.<sup id="cite_ref-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120-182">[182]</a></sup> An incident played into British hands when, while passing Aden for trading purposes, one of their sailing ships sank and Arab tribesmen boarded it and plundered its contents. The <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> government dispatched a warship under the command of Captain <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stafford_Bettesworth_Haines&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stafford Bettesworth Haines (page does not exist)">Stafford Bettesworth Haines</a> to demand compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120-182">[182]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Haines bombarded Aden from his warship in January 1839. The ruler of Lahej, who was in Aden at the time, ordered his guards to defend the port, but they failed in the face of overwhelming military and naval power. The British managed to occupy Aden and agreed to compensate the sultan with an annual payment of 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_rial" title="Yemeni rial">riyals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120-182">[182]</a></sup> The British evicted the Sultan of Lahej from Aden and forced him to accept their "protection."<sup id="cite_ref-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120_182-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caesar_E._Farah_2002_120-182">[182]</a></sup> In November 1839, 5000 tribesmen tried to retake the town but were repulsed and 200 were killed. The British realised that Aden's prosperity depended on their relations with the neighbouring tribes, which required that they rest on a firm and satisfactory basis.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The British government concluded "protection and friendship" treaties with nine tribes surrounding Aden, whereas they would remain independent from British interference in their affairs as long as they do not conclude treaties with foreigners (non-Arab colonial powers).<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Aden was declared a <a href="/wiki/Free_economic_zone" title="Free economic zone">free zone</a> in 1850. With emigrants from India, East Africa, and Southeast Asia, Aden grew into a world city. In 1850, only 980 Arabs were registered as original inhabitants of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The English presence in Aden put them at odds with the Ottomans. The Turks asserted to the British that they held sovereignty over the whole of Arabia, including Yemen as the successor of Mohammed and the Chief of the Universal Caliphate.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_return">Ottoman return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ottoman return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Vilayet" title="Yemen Vilayet">Yemen Vilayet</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg/170px-Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg/255px-Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg/340px-Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="630"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mukhtar_Pasha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Grand_Vizier" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Vizier">Grand Vizier</a> and <a href="/wiki/W%C4%81li" class="mw-redirect" title="WÄli">WÄli</a> of Yemen <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Muhtar_Pasha" title="Ahmed Muhtar Pasha">Ahmed Muhtar Pasha</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Ottomans were concerned about the British expansion from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and Arabia. They returned to the Tihama in 1849 after an absence of two centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-187">[187]</a></sup> Rivalries and disturbances continued among the Zaydi imams, between them and their deputies, with the <a href="/wiki/Ulema" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulema">ulema</a>, with the heads of tribes, as well as with those who belonged to other sects. Some citizens of Sana'a were desperate to return law and order to Yemen and asked the Ottoman Pasha in Tihama to pacify the country.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Yemeni merchants knew that the return of the Ottomans would improve their trade, for the Ottomans would become their customers.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> An Ottoman expedition force tried to capture Sana'a, but was defeated and had to evacuate the highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The Opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in 1869, strengthened the Ottoman decision to remain in Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-B._Z._Eraqi_Klorman_1993_11_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B._Z._Eraqi_Klorman_1993_11-191">[191]</a></sup> In 1872, military forces were dispatched from Constantinople and moved beyond the Ottoman stronghold in the lowlands (Tihama) to conquer Sana'a. By 1873, the Ottomans succeeded in conquering the northern highlands. Sana'a became the administrative capital of <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Vilayet" title="Yemen Vilayet">Yemen Vilayet</a>. </p>
      <p>The Ottomans learned from their previous experience and worked on the disempowerment of local lords in the highland regions. They even attempted to secularize the Yemeni society, while <a href="/wiki/Yemenite_Jews" title="Yemenite Jews">Yemenite Jews</a> came to perceive themselves in Yemeni nationalist terms.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> The Ottomans appeased the tribes by forgiving their rebellious chiefs and appointing them to administrative posts. They introduced a series of reforms to enhance the country's economic welfare. However, corruption was widespread in the Ottoman administration in Yemen. This was because only the worst of the officials were appointed because those who could avoid serving in Yemen did so.<sup id="cite_ref-DoÄan_GÃ¼rpÄ±nar_2013_71_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoÄan_GÃ¼rpÄ±nar_2013_71-193">[193]</a></sup> The Ottomans had reasserted control over the highlands for a temporary duration.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-187">[187]</a></sup> The so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a></i> reforms were considered heretic by the Zaydi tribes. In 1876, the Hashid and Bakil tribes rebelled against the Ottomans; the Turks had to appease them with gifts to end the uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The tribal chiefs were difficult to appease and an endless cycle of violence curbed Ottoman efforts to pacify the land. <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Izzet_Pasha" title="Ahmed Izzet Pasha">Ahmed Izzet Pasha</a> proposed that the Ottoman army evacuate the highlands and confine itself to Tihama, and not unnecessarily burden itself with continuing military operation against the Zaydi tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-DoÄan_GÃ¼rpÄ±nar_2013_71_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoÄan_GÃ¼rpÄ±nar_2013_71-193">[193]</a></sup> The hit-and-run tactics of the northern highlands tribesmen wore out the Ottoman military. They resented the Turkish <i>Tanzimat</i> and defied all attempts to impose a central government upon them.<sup id="cite_ref-B._Z._Eraqi_Klorman_1993_11_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B._Z._Eraqi_Klorman_1993_11-191">[191]</a></sup> The northern tribes united under the leadership of the House of Hamidaddin in 1890. Imam <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Muhammad_Hamid_ed-Din" title="Yahya Muhammad Hamid ed-Din">Yahya Hamidaddin</a> led a rebellion against the Turks in 1904; the rebels disrupted the Ottoman ability to govern.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> The revolts between 1904 and 1911 were especially damaging to the Ottomans, costing them as many as 10,000 soldiers and as much as 500,000 <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pounds</a> per year.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> The Ottomans signed a treaty with imam <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Muhammad_Hamid_ed-Din" title="Yahya Muhammad Hamid ed-Din">Yahya Hamidaddin</a> in 1911. Under the treaty, Imam Yahya was recognized as an autonomous leader of the Zaydi northern highlands. The Ottomans continued to rule <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a> areas in the mid-south until their departure in 1918. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutawakkilite_Kingdom_of_Yemen">Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mutawakkilite_Kingdom_of_Yemen" title="Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen">Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Dar_al_hajar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Dar_al_hajar.jpg/170px-Dar_al_hajar.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Dar_al_hajar.jpg/255px-Dar_al_hajar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Dar_al_hajar.jpg/340px-Dar_al_hajar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Dar_al_hajar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Imam <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Muhammad_Hamid_ed-Din" title="Yahya Muhammad Hamid ed-Din">Yahya Hamid Ed-Din</a>'s house near Sana'a
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><b>Imam Yahya hamid ed-Din al-Mutawakkil</b> was ruling the northern highlands independently from 1911. After the Ottoman departure in 1918, he sought to recapture the lands of his Qasimid ancestors. He dreamed of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Yemen" title="Greater Yemen">Greater Yemen</a> stretching from Asir to Dhofar. These schemes brought him into conflict with the <i>de facto</i> rulers in the territories claimed, namely the <a href="/wiki/Idrisid_Emirate_of_Asir" title="Idrisid Emirate of Asir">Idrisids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">Ibn Saud</a>, and the British government in Aden.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> The Zaydi imam did not recognize the Anglo-Ottoman border agreement of 1905 on the grounds that it was made between two foreign powers occupying Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> The border treaty effectively divided Yemen into north and south.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> In 1915, the British signed a treaty with the <a href="/wiki/Idrisid_Emirate_of_Asir" title="Idrisid Emirate of Asir">Idrisids</a> guaranteeing their security and independence if they would fight against the Turks.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> In 1919, Imam Yahya hamid ed-Din moved southward to "liberate" the nine British protectorates. The British responded by moving quickly towards Tihama and occupying <a href="/wiki/Al_Hudaydah" title="Al Hudaydah">al-Hudaydah</a>. Then they handed it over to their Idrisi allies.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Imam <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Muhammad_Hamid_ed-Din" title="Yahya Muhammad Hamid ed-Din">Yahya</a> attacked the southern protectorates again in 1922. The British bombed Yahya's tribal forces using aircraft to which the tribes had no effective counter.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated34_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated34-202">[202]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1925, Imam Yahya captured al-Hudaydah from the Idrisids.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated509_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated509-203">[203]</a></sup> He continued to follow and attack the Idrisids until Asir fell under the control of the imam's forces, forcing the Idrisi to request an agreement that would enable them to administer the region in the name of the imam.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated509_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated509-203">[203]</a></sup> Imam Yahya refused the offer on the grounds that the Idrisis were of Moroccan descent. According to Imam Yahya, the Idrisis, along with the British, were nothing but recent intruders and should be driven out of Yemen permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> In 1927, Imam Yahya's forces were about 50&nbsp;km (30&nbsp;mi) away from Aden, Taiz, and Ibb, and were bombed by the British for five days; the imam had to pull back.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated34_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated34-202">[202]</a></sup> Small <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> forces, mainly from the <a href="/wiki/Madh%27hij" title="Madh'hij">Madh'hij</a> confederation of <a href="/wiki/Marib" class="mw-redirect" title="Marib">Marib</a>, attacked <a href="/wiki/Shabwah_Governorate" title="Shabwah Governorate">Shabwah</a> but were bombed by the British and had to retreat. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Empire" title="Italian Empire">Italian Empire</a> was the first to recognize Imam Yahya as the King of Yemen in 1926. This created a great deal of anxiety for the British, who interpreted it as recognition of Imam Yahya's claim to sovereignty over Greater Yemen, which included the <a href="/wiki/Aden_protectorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Aden protectorate">Aden protectorate</a> and Asir.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The Idrisis turned to <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">Ibn Saud</a> seeking his protection from Yahya Muhammad Hamid ed-Din. However, in 1932, the Idrisis broke their accord with Ibn Saud and went back to Imam Yahya seeking help against Ibn Saud himself, who had begun liquidating their authority and expressed his desire to annex those territories into his own Saudi domain.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Imam Yahya demanded the return of all Idrisi dominion.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101-206">[206]</a></sup> That same year, a group of Hejazi liberals fled to Yemen and plotted to expel Ibn Saud from the former <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hejaz" title="Kingdom of Hejaz">Hashemite Kingdom of Hejaz</a>, which had been <a href="/wiki/Saudi_conquest_of_Hejaz" title="Saudi conquest of Hejaz">conquered by the Saudis</a> seven years earlier. Ibn Saud appealed to Britain for aid.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97-208">[208]</a></sup> The British government sent arms and aeroplanes .<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97-208">[208]</a></sup> The British were anxious that Ibn Saud's financial difficulties may encourage the Italian Empire to bail him out.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_2002_101-206">[206]</a></sup> Ibn Saud suppressed the Asiri rebellion in 1933, after which the Idrisids fled to Sana'a.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97-208">[208]</a></sup> Negotiations between the Imam Yahya Hamid ed-Din and Ibn Saud proved fruitless. After a military confrontation, Ibn Saud announced a ceasefire in May 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97_208-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madawi_al-Rasheed_97-208">[208]</a></sup> Imam Yahya agreed to release Saudi hostages and the surrender of the Idrisis to Saudi custody. Imam Yahya ceded the three provinces of Najran, Asir, and <a href="/wiki/Jizan_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Jizan Region">Jazan</a> for 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> and signed another treaty with the British government in 1934. The imam recognized the British sovereignty over Aden protectorate for 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Out of fear for <a href="/wiki/Al_Hudaydah" title="Al Hudaydah">Hudaydah</a>, Yahya did submit to these demands. According to Bernard Reich, Professor of Political Science and International Affairs at <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a>, Yahya could have done better by reorganizing the Zaydi tribes of the northern highlands as his ancestors did against the Turks and British intruders and turn the lands they captured into another graveyard.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Aden">Colonial Aden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Colonial Aden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg/170px-Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg/255px-Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg/340px-Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg 2x" data-file-width="494" data-file-height="784"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_in_Aden_1954.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> holding a sword, prepared to <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knight</a> subjects in <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> in 1954
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Starting in 1890, hundreds of Yemeni people from Hajz, Al-Baetha, and Taiz migrated to Aden to work at ports, and as labourers. This helped the population of Aden once again become predominantly Arab after, having been declared a free zone, it had become mostly foreigners. During World War II, Aden had increasing economic growth and became the second-busiest port in the world after <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wealth_p.117_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wealth_p.117-212">[212]</a></sup> After the rise of labour unions, a rift was apparent between the sectors of workers and the first signs of resistance to the occupation started in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-Wealth_p.117_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wealth_p.117-212">[212]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Luqman" title="Muhammad Ali Luqman">Muhammad Ali Luqman</a> founded the first Arabic club and school in <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a>, and was the first to start working towards a union.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Aden" class="mw-redirect" title="Colony of Aden">Colony of Aden</a> was divided into an eastern colony and a western colony. Those were further divided into 23 sultanates and emirates, and several independent tribes that had no relationships with the sultanates. The deal between the sultanates and Britain detailed protection and complete control of foreign relations by the British. The Sultanate of Lahej was the only one in which the sultan was referred to as <i>His Highness</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_South_Arabia" title="Federation of South Arabia">Federation of South Arabia</a> was created by the British to counter <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a> by giving more freedom to the rulers of the nations.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">North Yemen Civil War</a> inspired many in the south to rise against the British rule. The <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Yemen)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Liberation Front (Yemen)">National Liberation Front</a> (NLF) of Yemen was formed with the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Qahtan_Muhammad_al-Shaabi" title="Qahtan Muhammad al-Shaabi">Qahtan Muhammad Al-Shaabi</a>. The NLF hoped to destroy all the sultanates and eventually unite with the <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a>. Most of the support for the NLF came from <a href="/wiki/Radfan" title="Radfan">Radfan</a> and Yafa, so the British launched Operation Nutcracker, which completely burned Radfan in January 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_states">Two states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Two states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg/220px-Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg/330px-Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg/440px-Egyptian_army_in_sanaa_1962.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="553"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">Egyptian military intervention</a> in North Yemen, 1962
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Divided_Yemen.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Divided_Yemen.svg/220px-Divided_Yemen.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Divided_Yemen.svg/330px-Divided_Yemen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Divided_Yemen.svg/440px-Divided_Yemen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1210" data-file-height="730"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a> (in orange) and <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a> (in blue) before 1990
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Arab nationalism made an impact in some circles who opposed the lack of modernization efforts in the <b>Mutawakkilite monarchy</b>. This became apparent when Imam <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_bin_Yahya" title="Ahmad bin Yahya">Ahmad bin Yahya</a> died in 1962. He was succeeded by his son, but army officers attempted to seize power, sparking the <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">North Yemen Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> The Hamidaddin royalists were supported by Saudi Arabia, Britain, and Jordan (mostly with weapons and financial aid, but also with small military forces), whilst the military rebels were backed by Egypt. Egypt provided the rebels with weapons and financial assistance, but also sent a large military force to participate in the fighting. Israel covertly supplied weapons to the royalists to keep the Egyptian military busy in Yemen and make Nasser less likely to initiate a conflict in the Sinai. After six years of civil war, the military rebels were victorious (February 1968) and formed the <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The revolution in the north coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Aden_Emergency" title="Aden Emergency">Aden Emergency</a>, which hastened the end of British rule in the south. On 30 November 1967, the state of South Yemen was formed, comprising Aden and the former Protectorate of South Arabia. This socialist state was later officially known as the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Republic_of_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Democratic Republic of Yemen">People's Democratic Republic of Yemen</a> and a programme of nationalisation was begun.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Aden7-1967.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Aden7-1967.jpg/220px-Aden7-1967.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Aden7-1967.jpg/330px-Aden7-1967.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Aden7-1967.jpg/440px-Aden7-1967.jpg 2x" data-file-width="813" data-file-height="602"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Aden7-1967.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/British_Forces_Aden" title="British Forces Aden">British Army</a>'s counter-insurgency campaign in the British-controlled territories of <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_South_Arabia" title="Federation of South Arabia">South Arabia</a>, 1967
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Relations between the two Yemeni states fluctuated between peaceful and hostile. The South was supported by the Eastern bloc. The North, however, was not able to get the same connections. In 1972, the two states fought a war. The war was resolved with a ceasefire and negotiations brokered by the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, where it was declared that unification would eventually occur. In 1978, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Abdullah_Saleh" title="Ali Abdullah Saleh">Ali Abdullah Saleh</a> was named as president of the Yemen Arab Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> After the war, the North complained about the South's help from foreign countries. This included Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1979, fresh fighting between the two states resumed and efforts were renewed to bring about unification.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Thousands were killed in 1986 in the <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen_Civil_War" title="South Yemen Civil War">South Yemen Civil War</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Ali_Nasser_Muhammad" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Nasser Muhammad">Ali Nasser Muhammad</a> fled to the north and was later sentenced to death for treason. A new government formed.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><br> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unification_and_civil_war">Unification and civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Unification and civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_unification" title="Yemeni unification">Yemeni unification</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1990, the two governments reached a full agreement on the joint governing of Yemen, and the countries were merged on 22 May 1990, with Saleh as President.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> The President of South Yemen, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Salim_al-Beidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Salim al-Beidh">Ali Salim al-Beidh</a>, became Vice President.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> A unified <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_Representatives_of_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Assembly of Representatives of Yemen">parliament</a> was formed and a unity constitution was agreed upon.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Timeline_220-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Timeline-220">[220]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_parliamentary_election,_1993" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni parliamentary election, 1993">1993 parliamentary election</a>, the first held after unification, the <a href="/wiki/General_People%27s_Congress_(Yemen)" title="General People's Congress (Yemen)">General People's Congress</a> won 122 of 301 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-elections_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elections-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>309</span></sup> </p>
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">invasion of Kuwait</a> crisis in 1990, Yemen's president opposed military intervention from non-Arab states.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> As a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> for 1990 and 1991, Yemen abstained on a number of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">UNSC</a> resolutions concerning Iraq and Kuwait<sup id="cite_ref-YemenProfile2008_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YemenProfile2008-224">[224]</a></sup> and voted against the "...use of force resolution." The vote outraged the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Saudi Arabia expelled 800,000 Yemenis in 1990 and 1991 to punish Yemen for its opposition to the intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the absence of strong state institutions, <a href="/wiki/Elite_theory" title="Elite theory">elite politics</a> in Yemen constituted a <i>de facto</i> form of <a href="/wiki/Collaborative_governance" title="Collaborative governance">collaborative governance</a>, where competing tribal, regional, religious, and political interests agreed to hold themselves in check through tacit acceptance of the balance it produced.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> The informal political settlement was held together by a power-sharing deal among three men: President Saleh, who controlled the state; major general <a href="/wiki/Ali_Mohsen_al-Ahmar" title="Ali Mohsen al-Ahmar">Ali Mohsen al-Ahmar</a>, who controlled the largest share of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Yemen_Armed_Forces" title="Republic of Yemen Armed Forces">Republic of Yemen Armed Forces</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Husayn_al-Ahmar" title="Abdullah ibn Husayn al-Ahmar">Abdullah ibn Husayn al-Ahmar</a>, figurehead of the Islamist <a href="/wiki/Al-Islah_(Yemen)" title="Al-Islah (Yemen)">al-Islah party</a> and Saudi Arabia's chosen broker of transnational <a href="/wiki/Clientelism" title="Clientelism">patronage payments</a> to various political players,<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> including tribal <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikhs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> The Saudi payments have been intended to facilitate the tribes' autonomy from the Yemeni government and to give the Saudi government a mechanism with which to weigh in on Yemen's political decision-making.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following food riots in major towns in 1992, a new coalition government made up of the ruling parties from both the former Yemeni states was formed in 1993. However, Vice President al-Beidh withdrew to Aden in August 1993 and said he would not return to the government until his grievances were addressed. These included northern violence against his <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Socialist_Party" title="Yemeni Socialist Party">Yemeni Socialist Party</a>, as well as the economic marginalization of the south.<sup id="cite_ref-war_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-war-234">[234]</a></sup> Negotiations to end the political deadlock dragged on into 1994. The government of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Haydar_Abu_Bakr_Al-Attas" class="mw-redirect" title="Haydar Abu Bakr Al-Attas">Haydar Abu Bakr Al-Attas</a> became ineffective due to political infighting<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>An accord between northern and southern leaders was signed in <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> on 20 February 1994, but this could not stop the civil war.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During these tensions, both the northern and southern armies (which had never integrated) gathered on their respective frontiers.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The May â July <a href="/wiki/1994_civil_war_in_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="1994 civil war in Yemen">1994 civil war in Yemen</a> resulted in the defeat of the southern armed forces and the flight into exile of many Yemeni Socialist Party leaders and other southern secessionists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Saudi Arabia actively aided the south during the 1994 civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_Yemen">Contemporary Yemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contemporary Yemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">

          <div class="thumbcaption">
           "Sana'a risks becoming the first capital in the world to <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Yemen" title="Water supply and sanitation in Yemen">run out of a viable water supply</a> as Yemen's streams and natural aquifers run dry," says <i>The Guardian</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg/220px-Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg/330px-Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg/440px-Destroyed_house_in_the_south_of_Sanaa_12-6-2015-4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4128" data-file-height="3096"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi-led air strike</a> on Sana'a, 12 June 2015: Saudi Arabia is operating in violation of international law
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Ali_Abdullah_Saleh" title="Ali Abdullah Saleh">Ali Abdullah Saleh</a> became Yemen's first directly elected president in <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_presidential_election,_1999" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni presidential election, 1999">the 1999 presidential election</a>, winning 96.2 per cent of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-elections_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elections-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>310</span></sup> The only other candidate, <a href="/wiki/Najeeb_Qahtan_Al-Sha%27abi" title="Najeeb Qahtan Al-Sha'abi">Najeeb Qahtan Al-Sha'abi</a>, was the son of Qahtan Muhammad al-Sha'abi, a former president of <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a>. Though a member of Saleh's <a href="/wiki/General_People%27s_Congress_(Yemen)" title="General People's Congress (Yemen)">General People's Congress</a> (GPC) party, Najeeb ran as an independent.<sup id="cite_ref-REVERSAL_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REVERSAL-239">[239]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In October 2000, 17 U.S. personnel died after a <a href="/wiki/USS_Cole_bombing" title="USS Cole bombing">suicide attack on the U.S. naval vessel USS <i>Cole</i></a> in Aden, which was subsequently blamed on al-Qaeda. After the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> on the United States, President Saleh assured U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> that Yemen was a partner in his <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" class="mw-redirect" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>. In 2001, violence surrounded <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_constitutional_referendum,_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni constitutional referendum, 2001">a referendum</a>, which apparently supported extending Saleh's rule and powers. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Shia_insurgency_in_Yemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia insurgency in Yemen">Shia insurgency in Yemen</a> began in June 2004 when dissident cleric <a href="/wiki/Hussein_Badreddin_al-Houthi" title="Hussein Badreddin al-Houthi">Hussein Badreddin al-Houthi</a>, head of the Zaidi Shia sect, launched an uprising against the Yemeni government. The Yemeni government alleged that the <a href="/wiki/Houthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Houthis">Houthis</a> were seeking to overthrow it and to implement <i>ShÄ«'ite</i> <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious law</a>. The rebels counter that they are "defending their community against discrimination" and government aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-Deadly_blast_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deadly_blast-240">[240]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2005, at least 36 people were killed in clashes across the country between police and protesters over rising fuel prices. </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_presidential_election,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni presidential election, 2006">2006 presidential election</a>, held on 20 September, Saleh won with 77.2% of the vote. His main rival, <a href="/wiki/Faisal_bin_Shamlan" class="mw-redirect" title="Faisal bin Shamlan">Faisal bin Shamlan</a>, received 21.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-presidentsaleh.gov.ye_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidentsaleh.gov.ye-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> Saleh was sworn in for another term on 27 September.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A suicide bomber killed eight Spanish tourists and two Yemenis in the <a href="/wiki/Ma%27rib_Governorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma'rib Governorate">province of Marib</a> in July 2007. A series of bomb attacks occurred on police, official, diplomatic, foreign business, and tourism targets in 2008. Car bombings outside the U.S. embassy in Sana'a killed 18 people, including six of the assailants in September 2008. In 2008, an opposition rally in Sana'a demanding electoral reform was met with police gunfire. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Al-Qaeda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In January 2009, the Saudi Arabian and Yemeni al-Qaeda branches merged to form <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Arabian_Peninsula" title="Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula">Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula</a>, which is based in Yemen, and many of its members were Saudi nationals who had been released from Guantanamo Bay.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> Saleh released 176 al-Qaeda suspects on condition of good behaviour, but terrorist activities continued. </p>
      <p>The Yemeni army launched a fresh offensive against the Shia insurgents in 2009, assisted by Saudi forces. Tens of thousands of people were displaced by the fighting. A new ceasefire was agreed upon in February 2010. However, by the end of the year, Yemen claimed that 3,000 soldiers had been killed in renewed fighting. The Shia rebels accused Saudi Arabia of providing support to <a href="/wiki/Salafism" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafism">salafi groups</a> to suppress <a href="/wiki/Zaidism" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaidism">Zaidism</a> in Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On orders from U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, U.S. warplanes fired <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a> at what officials in Washington claimed were Al Qaeda training camps in the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Sana%27a_Governorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sana'a Governorate">Sana'a</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abyan" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyan">Abyan</a> on 17 December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> Instead of hitting Al-Qaeda operatives, it hit a village, killing 55 civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-foreignpolicy.com_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreignpolicy.com-247">[247]</a></sup> Officials in Yemen said that the attacks claimed the lives of more than 60 civilians, 28 of them children. Another airstrike was carried out on 24 December.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The U.S. launched a series of <a href="/wiki/Disposition_Matrix" title="Disposition Matrix">drone attacks in Yemen</a> to curb a perceived growing terror threat due to political chaos in Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Since December 2009, U.S. strikes in Yemen have been carried out by the U.S. military with intelligence support from the CIA.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> The drone strikes are protested by human-rights groups who say they kill innocent civilians, and that the U.S. military and CIA drone strikes lack sufficient congressional oversight, including the choice of human targets suspected of being threats to America.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> Controversy over U.S. policy for drone attacks mushroomed after a September 2011 drone strike in Yemen killed <a href="/wiki/Anwar_al-Awlaki" title="Anwar al-Awlaki">Anwar al-Awlaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samir_Khan" title="Samir Khan">Samir Khan</a>, both U.S. citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> Another drone strike in October 2011 killed Anwar's teenage son, <a href="/wiki/Abdulrahman_al-Awlaki" title="Abdulrahman al-Awlaki">Abdulrahman al-Awlaki</a>. </p>
      <p>In 2010, the Obama administration policy allowed targeting of people whose names are not known. The U.S. government increased military aid to $140&nbsp;million in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> U.S. drone strikes continued after the ousting of President Saleh.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Shi'a</a> <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Committee_(Yemen)" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary Committee (Yemen)">Houthis</a> are fighting against the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> Al Qaeda,<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> and Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> The U.S. supports the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi-led military intervention in Yemen</a> against the Houthis,<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> but many in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Special_Operations_Command" title="United States Special Operations Command">US SOCOM</a> reportedly favor <a href="/wiki/Houthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Houthis">Houthis</a>, as they have been an effective force to roll back al-Qaeda and recently ISIL in Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> reported that "The only groups poised to benefit from the war dragging on are the jihadis of Islamic State (ISIL) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Arabian_Peninsula" title="Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula">Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula</a> (AQAP), the latter's most powerful franchise, who are likely to gain influence amid the chaos. ISIL has claimed recent, bloody <a href="/wiki/2015_Sana%27a_mosque_bombings" class="mw-redirect" title="2015 Sana'a mosque bombings">suicide bombings</a> in Houthi mosques and Sana'a when it once had no known presence in the country, while AQAP has continued to seize territory in eastern Yemen unhindered by American drone strikes."<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> In February 2016 Al-Qaeda forces and Saudi-led coalition forces were both seen fighting Houthi rebels in the same battle.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20160222_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20160222-261">[261]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In June 2019, the leader of ISIS in Yemen, Abu Osama al-Muhajir, was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen</a> supported by the United States during a raid in the province of al-Mahra.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> The operation included Yemeni security forces and recovered a number of weapons, ammunition, computers, money in different currencies and communications equipment. It did not injure any civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution_and_aftermath">Revolution and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Revolution and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
